39:["$","p",null,{"children":["The unitary gates in our default collection form what's called a ",["$","em",null,{"children":"universal"}]," gate set.\nThis means that we can approximate any unitary operation on any number of qubits to any degree of accuracy we wish, using circuits composed of these gates alone.\nTo be clear, the definition of universality places no requirements on the cost of such approximations, meaning the number of gates from our set that we need.\nIndeed, a fairly simple argument based on the mathematical notion of measure reveals that most unitary operations must have extremely high cost.\nProving the universality of quantum gate sets is not a simple matter and won't be covered in this course."],"className":"group paragraph-el text-text-primary mx-0"}]

48:["$","p",null,{"children":["But notice that the size of a circuit doesn't necessarily correspond directly to how long it takes to run.\nIn our Boolean circuit for computing the XOR of two bits, for instance, the two FANOUT gates could be performed simultaneously, as could the two NOT gates, as well as the two AND gates.\nA different way to measure the efficiency of circuits, which takes this possibility of ",["$","em",null,{"children":"parallelization"}]," into account, is by their ",["$","em",null,{"children":"depth"}],".\nThis is the minimum number of ",["$","em",null,{"children":"layers"}]," of gates needed within the circuit, where the gates within each layer operate on different wires.\nEquivalently, the depth of a circuit is the maximum number of gates encountered on any path from an input wire to an output wire.\nFor the circuit above, for instance, the depth is 4."],"className":"group paragraph-el text-text-primary mx-0"}]
